Presentation matters, and if these issues are as important to us as we say, then it should be just as important to us to present them to people in an appealing and comprehensible way if we want people to truly understand their importance and want to support them. Besides, while fighting against people who seem to be all about vitriol and gatekeeping, it's important to me at least to genuinely not be the same negative, angry, vitriolic people as they are. If we're aiming to create togetherness we can't be fighting amongst each other over things like who suffered more, and who's a true paragon of morality and altruism. We need to look at people realistically and not cannibalize people who may have made mistakes in their past, especially if they're turning their lives around and now want to support progressive causes, and not demonize people who support progressive ideals but who also enjoy material wealth or just plain realize they NEED money to be able to put some of their progressive ideas in motion. Nobody's perfect, nobody's an angel or a saint. We're just people trying to shape our world into one where we can be a little happier before we die, so we need to shift this from parsing good and evil and focus more on what goals we need to achieve to create the world we're hoping to live in.

It's not necessarily that anti-SJWs are unpopular or uncool, that's beside the point. The issue is how SJWs have been represented, as being evil. SJWs are made out to be this group of sociopaths that are preying on weaker men to push a women's agenda, and as black people lying about their disenfranchisement. If not that, they're made to seem incorrect in other ways.  For example taking a discussion about race; specifically are black Americans more likely to commit crimes? This is a subject that requires competency and research to establish the truth on, with an immense amount of pressure to report accurately due to the consequences of an erroneous conclusion. If you come to the conclusion that black people tend to commit more crimes, then you are interpreted as a racist rather than merely wrong. And this is also a problem because the person doing it is indeed wise enough to know that their conclusions may also be used by racists to try to justify their own dogma and racist worldviews.  The thing about trying to learn the truth is that it's significantly harder. Truth isn't something that the world just gives to people for free. Especially in statistical analysis, and liars also confound the issues. As now everyone has to take time out of their own lives to spend researching new facts, in order not to be racists, if they even are the kind of people who care if someone calls them racist.  And after a certain point people start to take shortcuts. They decide "well in the absence of opposition and more and more people reinforcing my viewpoints here, and also in addition to that, only seeing strawmen of the opposition, I might as well accept black people commit more crimes."  It can take a lot of time to get to the point where you understand why the sentence "black people commit more crime" doesn't indicate that they should be given more attention by the police. But when you figure it out, it's a magic moment. Of course by that point the amount of time spent defending yourself amidst being labelled has produced a lot of fuck ups. My only critique of this is that I think you painted with too broad a brush. Your caricature of the left is a homolgomation of aynsyns, trots, SJWs, and I guess leftcoms?  There is little utility in painting "the left" with so broad a brush. We're talking about schools of thought that fundamentally disagree on both methods and motives. There will never be "left unity" because "the left" is defined only by our opposition to "the right," a term nearly as vague as its opposite. The left will never agree on how to confront the problem because we disagree on what the problem is, and we also fundamentally disagree on the best way to go about combating the problem.   It's okay to be specific about who you are and who you oppose. I'm a Marxist Leninist and I am opposed to Imperialism above all else because I believe that imperialism is the greatest barrier to working class power, and I believe that all the specific forms of oppression are expressions of imperialism. Therefore, to dismantle bigotry, poverty, etc, we have to confront the root of the problem. This belief and approach differs on a fundamental level from the beliefs and approaches of anarchists, leftcoms, trots, etc. Anarchists, for example, barely (if ever) recognize the existence of imperialism at all.  I think this video is pretty good overall as its criticisms of certain elements of the left ring true. We do need to be doing a better job of cultivating an image that is appealing to the masses. We also need to do a better job of meeting people where they're at instead of demanding people abandon their fundamental beliefs at the drop of a hat. All that being said, however, I felt that the video fell a bit flat on how to move forward. Simply improving PR will not move us forward. Only class struggle can do that. I agree with Boots Riley for the most part on this. We have to prove to the masses that our methods work by winning real material victories, not symbolic ones. Only then can we begin to build a working class movement. An appealing public image is a component of that, for sure, but without the class struggle component, it will be just as futile as knocking over trash cans.

On another note, as far as propaganda is concerned within the privileged, imperialist countries, even throughout the 20th century at the height of global communist revolution, it never happened in the centers of empire. As far as strategy is concerned, I think it may be better to focus on the polarization between the centers and peripheries of capitalism-imperialism, and this will redefine our goals into something much more sellable. By focusing on the imperialist nature of capitalism, our goals will inevitably change. And within the centers of empire, one of our goals can and must be anti-war. Anti-war is pretty easy to sell, I've found. This cause is more important in my opinion than the left fighting for universal healthcare or the nationalization of the banking industry, or whatever it is. Socialism is a scary term in US politics. But anti-war is not. Plus, organizing people around anti-war, a very sellable position, can help to increase the consciousness of the masses. We'll see. That's my strategy.  Also cringe at the anarcho-syndicalist calling someone else a bad leftist. Anarchists are great at punching Nazis, but their program is far too liberal and individualistic. Gotta focus on the masses!  And screw the "free marketplace of ideas." Just like all markets, the winners go to those who have wealth and power. We do seriously need to work on dismantling it if the Left wants to have any serious fighting chance. And it lets Nazis participate, because they have wealth and power.  Just my two cents

I found an interesting article that talks about the unpopularity of leftist thought. It's written for ancoms, but it really applies to every ideologue.  "When you separate politics from the immediate, everyday experiences of  individual men and women, it becomes completely irrelevant. Indeed, it  becomes the private domain of wealthy, comfortable intellectuals, who  can trouble themselves with such dreary, theoretical things. Finally, I've been wanting you to criticize Antifa for awhile now.
It shouldn't matter that sometimes a few Leftists are easy targets. You could make endless "cringe" compilations for both sides; fascists have their awkward members, and we have ours. The problem is that since this is very much a country sympathetic to fascism, those of us who are against it get held to a much, *much* higher standard of conduct. Fascists succeed by misbehaving, while leftists who do even a pale imitation of the same are held up as reasons why the essjaydubbayoos need to go.  So while anger is natural, and deserved, and while that anger should be on display when out in force for demonstrations, its inelegance is being used against us and we need additional strategy for the time being. I /have/ found that a "cool" tone works better when talking one on one.  A few articulate people staying calm and keeping an arsenal of statistics at the ready bolsters our image as a whole, and gives other left-wingers confidence. Basically, the Left needs to organize how we engage--not everyone can act detached, but designated mouthpieces can do it for them and serve the collective good in the process. It's not our anger, but rather our vulnerability, that will be discarded if we present an unflappable united front. On the other hand, I believe this emphasis on detachment earns us short-term brownie points while robbing us of the fire needed to truly right wrongs, because humans can never freaking reach a happy medium. Has politeness increased wages for Wal-Mart employees? Will it bring factory jobs back from Bangladesh where 12-year-olds now make our shirts? Shouldn't it give us pause that a respectful demeanor earns praise from the very people who benefit from the labor and deprivation of marginalized groups? Disobedience harms the disobedient, but serves the ones who come after.  Therefore, my take is that our mouthpieces should be politicians, which is why we need to reach firm consensus on candidates and get our asses to the polls, as Republicans have done with great success for decades. The onus of change can't be entirely placed on citizens; it's an elected representative's job, after all, to advocate in a level-headed manner. It's they who listen, they who amplify, and they who change laws. The main reason Democrats are so fractured is because we have no real faith in most of our guys, so we aren't a reliable source of support, therefore they turn corporate out of self-preservation, then we trust them even less, and on and on in a vicious circle. For all the criticisms being thrown at this video, the communist cat girl using queer as an insult? In my experience of leftist Twitter, that cat girl is probably at least trans, lesbian/bi/pansexual, polyamorous, surrounded by supportive followers calling her cute perpetually, and is likely a queer anarchist militant par excellance! It's naturally no coincidence that all of those signifiers apply to me in some capacity, and suffice it to say, I dive straight for deep cuts on all counts! I'm a trans NB woman, pansexual, and a relationship anarchist (yet have never been in a relationship/dated/sex'ed/kissed/unplatonic hugged/touched ungloved hands anyone); I have a adequate following that occasionally calls me cute when my anxiety doesn't push me away from Twitter for the sake of my mental stability, and it straight up isn't a revolution unless all the queers hurl the boot of systemic oppression into a black hole in the process of swallowing the sun and seize the right to be utterly nude wherever we dern well please, unless someone has trauma triggers stemming from genitalia, perhaps! All said, you're always welcome in the non-toxic dumpster fire part of socialist Twitter to learn true praxis! :D  That aside, I find the psychological dynamics on display between the two main characters of this installment to be fascinatingly fraught with dissonant self-critique. I find Gwen-no-Fear's observation that the Justine character, for all the self depreciation to be read into her lampshading of her arguably incoherent and ineffectual liberalism, is framed as a moral authority and teacher in the exchange in contrast to the more impulsive and toxic Tabby to be at least rather valid. Noting that, it seems that Justine could still be read as a more complicated character than that framing could potentially convey, leaving textual space to imagine a continuing internal conflict within Contra regarding her own perspective. This is also possibly illustrated by the... less charitable aspects of the Tabby character, intimating a ongoing process of forming a critical, if sympathetic, relationship with those currently further "left", as it were. I found the observation of pedantic and counter-productive sectarianism an accurate reflection of behaviors I've seen among internet "leftists". Mexie's even made a video dissecting this phenomenon and how it contrasts with her experiences of discourse involving leftist ideas in academia. However, I find that, on the whole, Contra seems to hold a perhaps exceedingly broad perspective on the prevalence of "excess" optimism and pessimism and attempts at accessibility by the left, at least in terms of her own claims regarding how she incorporated it into Tabby, likely the functional microcosm of leftism in this reading of the work. I grant acknowledgement of the presence of strains of both for fairness (Third-Worldists, posadists, and accelerationists for the former, and "Read a book!" lefties for the latter as but a few examples) and agree such behaviors are worthy of criticism in their own right, but the framing erases a good deal of nuance in recognition of activities, beliefs, and discourse that runs counter to behaviors presented here. While this is arguably in accordance with the intention of the character's function by the author, I question the efficacy of accentuating the negative to quite this degree in reaching leftists to whom some of the observations apply, but not others, or even those with problems that have yet to be addressed. The word "strawman" has already been attributed to the character a number of times, and I foresee it being used yet more. Despite that, while I don't find the term necessarily inaccurate myself, I also don't find it sufficient grounds for dismissal of all ideas conveyed by the character, as it could be easily argued that satire is necessarily reliant on strawmen by certain definitions.  So, as per usual, what was initially going to be a silly, cute joke ended up another indulgent treatise that took far too much time to write. Well, while I'm on my bullshit, some more indulgences. For the personal disadvantages that my psychological callouses and defense mechanisms cause, they do prove occasionally helpful in conveying the "ice-cool" demeanor in some internet conversations on these topics. Also, whenever I hear "breadlines and gulags" now, I tend to think of food stamps, homeless shelters, orphanages, and private prisons. Not that I'd by any means argue that the latter make the former excusable (I ain't no tankie!), but I find it helps put in perspective just how terrible capitalism is too not merely many, but most subjected to it. Approximately over a hundred million people continue to die every couple of years due to preventable causes because of private resource ownership after all, but y'all know that! Anyway, vive la commune and may gender transitions flow like water!

I agree with the brunt of the criticism ‚Äì the academic, white, Internet-based, memetic Left has a terrible image among the general public, and our general posture and attitude doesn't help.  I don't quite agree with the proposed solution.  It doesn't go deep enough.  The problem is, I think, deeper than simple optics, presentation, or propaganda strategies (consider Trump; no matter how stupid, funny, or ineffective he looks, his base remains unshakable; it's a good exercise to consider *why*). The problem is that the academic Left has disconnected itself from the workers, from the disenfranchised, from thieves and beggars and winos, from miners and construction people and streetwalkers and undocumented immigrant housemaids.  And it's easy to see why, and it's a hard problem to solve.  What does a privileged, middle-class anarcho-syndicalist trans catgirl have in common with your average opiod-crisis victim? How can they even approach each other, or speak the same language? And yet it's for them that leftist politics exist in the first place.  The task of connecting intellectual to worker is hard, but it's been done before; we'd do well to study how, to learn from history (e.g. the mass line), and then actually doing it.

While I'm "aware of optics" and think approaching people where they are is important, I don't like the wholesale dismissal of certain things here in the name of "optics". The hammer and sickle, for example, is a representation of urban and rural worker unity. The USSR put it on its flag, but it's not a USSR specific symbol any more than stars are American.  (Also the USSR massively reduced the incidence of famine conditions in its region. I avoid talking about this topic with "normies" but I think you can handle the truth, Contra: the Holodomor was a fascist propaganda spin on the last bad famine of the feudal era of Eastern Europe before it's modernisation, rather than a Communist failing or genocide attempt. I have a big problem with leftists who think that the only way to establish Socialism is through violent revolution. Whether they're MLM's, Trots, Anarchists, etc., a violent revolution is entirely infeasible. Back in 1930, sure, there weren't fighter jets, drones, tanks damn near impervious to anything except a Javelin missile, smart bombs, and fully automatic 40mm grenade launchers. In the time when violent revolutions happened, the revolutionaries were on relatively the same footing as the army, armaments wise. The Cuban army didn't have tanks or fighter-bombers (if they did, it was relatively few), the Francoists didn't have tanks or planes, tanks and planes weren't even that effective during the Russian civil war. When the military can sit in the comfort of their bases and drop missiles from drones so high up you can't see them, let alone hit them with a shoulder carry anti-aircraft weapon, your revolution is doomed without large numbers of people. We all saw what happened to the Iraqi military during the first Gulf War, they got fucking steam-rolled, an army that had been fighting the Iranians for years, destroyed in weeks by the United States. A violent revolution in the third-world, maybe that's not so far-fetched, but in the Imperial core, the "west", unless the military deserts their posts and comes to your aid, you are fucked. And hoping that the military will desert is a pipe dream. If we're going to win, it's going to be through electoral politics or a non-violent coup d'etat, such as when the Bolsheviks assumed power in the October Revolution, something only accomplishable via mass popular support  and massive strike action. Hearts and minds are extremely important, and you don't win hearts and minds by executing people without trial, however much they deserved it (looking at you Waltons).

I like this video a lot! It made me think and analyze my own and other's positions, and that's the most valuable thing. There's a weird line, and this video teases that line without ever crossing it, between tone policing (i.e. distracting from the topic at hand through critique of tone) and genuine criticism of how that tone affects people and how effective the argument is when presented that way. This is a very good and important discussion to have that usually ends up happening in the wrong place or at the wrong time, in the middle of some other point, but since this is framed as two liberals discussing the tones of their arguments, it bypasses concept of tone policing and gets right to the reasonable tone critique. This is most effective when talking to increasingly radical leftists like Tabby, of which I count myself among them, because the nature of the rhetoric can often do more harm than good, and I especially like the focus on the derision SJWs receive for being overly emotional, as well as the very true and reasonable counter that anger and emotions are the most natural and justified response to being discriminated against and disenfranchised. But in spite of that, it makes the larger and more uncomfortably true point that emotions and anger only work when you first have sympathy, or else they're just used to demonize you.  But it also lets you know the problems with tone can't really be solved using the complacent, subordinate position towards capitalism and authority Justine has, because those pretty but superfluous redesigns she comes up with to put a fun aesthetic pastiche over Marxist ideology are  just as "not cool" as anything she criticized Tabby for. Justine doesn't know what "cool" actually is either, and therefore is ultimately ineffective in a world where, oddly enough, due to our own history, both violent and nonviolent rebellion will always be considered "cool" compared to that kind of pastel complacency and deference to authority.  In simple terms - this seems almost like what I'd imagine a philosophical discussion between an Antifa extremist and Elizabeth Warren would be like, where I'm sitting here more like a Bernie Sanders - less willing to buy into that authoritarian hypocrisy in the system out of Warren, but wanting a political revolution that changes the system from the inside with the only violence being done as a matter of basic self-defense, not a willingly violent social revolution like what the most extreme Antifa leftists demand. Am I satisfied when a Nazi gets punched? Hell yeah, but that punch isn't going to help beyond just giving us that taste of satisfaction and bringing that one Nazi pain. Acknowledging the difference is HUGE if we want to succeed.

You can really tell contrapoints was a philosophy student, the way she designs these videos is brilliant. Instead of going out and just saying what she thinks, she performs a sort of socratic dialog between multiple conflicting inner viewpoints. The result is that all sides of the argument are presented in a balanced way, and you can't say you agree or disagree with what contrapoints says; you can only guess. You have to decide for yourself what the message of the video is, and that to me is part of the brilliance of these videos.

So there's a lot to unpack here... I mean yeah I've met leftists like this too. And I agree that we need the centrists (as evidenced by revolutionary history; if you don't have the centrists on your side, things don't tend to end well) so yes they need to be convinced. On the other hand I think there's also value in defending extreme measures. For example, breaking shop windows can terrify centrists and backfire, but on the other hand it can be a good idea to talk to them about it and make them question why broken shop windows scare them so much.   I mean giving in to the pressure to be respectable is actually also a problem the left has had. The left in the US especially (but also elsewhere) has been kind of a bunch of pushovers for a while now. Sometimes you do need to push your own narrative rather than to compromise. The tricky part is to figure out when is the time for which strategy. (Of course that's where studying history is a good idea.) But I think right now seems like a good moment to push a bit. I mean people are starting to realise that Nazis are still a thing and kind of a terrifying thing. President Trump is a reality now. Faith in the capitalist system is the lowest it's been for decades. I can't think of a more approproate moment in my entire lifetime for leftists to be assertive.  And I mean yes, if you can keep cool while you're angry, that helps. That's true. I haven't quite figured out how to do that yet though, and it's not an easy thing to do. It's an unfair standard to place on people. I would defend emotional outburst rather than tell people that it's not useful. I mean it might not convince YouTube shitlords or other arrogant bastards but it might still affect some people who are more sympathetic to people's distress.  I'm with you 100% on the obscure philosophy nonsense and horrible unintelligible jargon point though. Also frankly I still think the best tactics would be community building, raising class consciousness and encouraging activism (while being careful about the S and C words and especially the A word. Unless they come up in conversation of course.) Political arguments are important too but they only really matter for people who are already politically conscious. They shouldn't be the first priority.

Good video, and I mostly agree with you. You didn't raise it in your video, but ideologies do not create a revolution. Communism, as defined by Marx, is an objective class movement, where the working class seek to liberate themselves from their oppressors. During revolutionary times, ideology has not been the driving factor of the working class, it has instead been driven by the desire for freedom. The left has unfortunately been plagued by idealists who romanticise violence and controversial regimes. It is possible to hold the views of Justine whilst still seeking the end goal as Tabby. Personally, I'm not fully convinced that dressing in black to intimidate and use violence if necessary to keep nazis of our streets (It is really not to be negotiated if we are to allow white supremacy and other forms of xenophobia and violence), romanticising it is unnecessary and rather counter-productive. This video is difficult. Your frequent use of dialogue as a framing device, though dialectical af, makes it hard for such videos to make a single clear point. This is the point of course, the feeling it generates is frustration, and this can get one to think.   However, this one I found particularly difficult, since one of the characters felt closer to the intended viewpoint and so came across as preachy, especially since their smugness was built in as a character flaw.   While I agree with your criticism of the left's obtuseness and sectarianism, I think you overestimate their ineffectiveness or their unpopularity, since a lot of concrete leftist policies can be and have historically been popular. Socialising resources and democratic workplaces, these are not that hard to promote, and can at worst be called 'utopian'.   Lastly, the idea that the left needs better optics comes across as somewhat cynical to me. Actions don't literally speak for themselves, and yes this means that good publicity is important too. But still, if one sincerely believes that the left's cause is in the interest of 'the people', one should also at least somewhat believe that 'the people' can come to joing the cause of their own volition, without having to make it look cool.   In conclusion, I think fighting bad  representations of the left is more important than creating a cool version of it. In fact I think sincerity is more important than coolness. Maybe you can be both, but I don't see the point. I don't want people to be leftist solely because they find it cool. I want to see them join the left because they're motivated by love.

[OK so LONG ASS ATTEMPT AT A SYMPATHETIC VAGUELY MARXIST CRITIQUE OF THIS RATHER COOL VIDEO (READ AT YOUR OWN RISK)] So i have read your pinned comment too and i get what you mean : fascist propaganda is effective because it's simple, "bad ass" and based on prejudices (mostly white) people have and it adapts to the latest cultural fashion, while leftist propaganda does not often do that (or when it does, it is actually centre-left politics IMO). Not only does the left needs to become "cooler" as you put it, but i would add it needs to move beyond soviet posters. Not that i am against, I think they are rather stylish and have nice colors sometimes, but I have noticed (at least online) is that the people who share it are *already* convinced by the necessity of a revolution. It does not touch the heart of your average person.   In the other hand, i think there is a reason WHY these symbols are so important. They represent the height of a struggle in the past, and the desire to reach the same result in the future : a world free of fascism, capitalism and imperialism, where everyone's needs are met. So i don't think the left should give up on the hammer and sickle and the like, but rather : -reappropriate them (in the like of "laborwave stuff")  -most importantly : EXPLAIN THEM. most people have a nasty view of communism because of capitalist propaganda. i'm not saying it's easy to open your eyes to the reality of capitalism, but i think an *organized* working class (call me old fashioned, but the centralized party that is still able to criticize itself and learn form its mistakes (maoist style) is still the best way to achieve this) could have the power to grow its support base by printing GOOD leftist newspapers (both online and offline)  and also teaching its supporters to NOT BEHAVE LIKE PRICKS online (because indeed, the aesthetics of your average SJW is 30% queer (loving that as a bi girl) but also 30% "i'm right because i'm oppressed" and 40% CRINGE.).  -adapt them to what people will relate to. Lenin and Marx were great, we need to read their books, but people of color might be more touched by the Black Panther Party or Francis Fanon. LGBT+ folks might care too if you mention radical queer groups who tried to bring together marxism and LGBT struggles. same if we mention everything that was achieved for women under socialism (and is still being done is zones of India that are controlled by the red guerrilla as i write)   It would also make street level antifascism more effective because a) people would understand the necessity of punching nazis and b) an organized party would prevent the white working class from falling for fascist rethoric by providing them the militant and theorical tools they need. Many communists would agree that street level antifascism is necessary in the sense that it is the chemo against the cancer of fascism, but as always, prevention is key.    All of this would be a lot more effective than memes than non-leftists either don't get or are afraid of. Memes can be good propaganda but you need a political and artistic subtlety that a lot of online activist lack. you can actually see that, weirdly enough on... reddit. WELL not /pol obviously, but r/communism101, even though it has its problems, is one of the few places on the internet where people who are curious about communism and other left-wing ideologies without (yet) 100% agreeing with it can get an answer without getting straight-up capitalist propaganda  or getting scolded by pretentious assholes. you also have a few folks like Usul, a French youtuber who makes leftist content that is relatable because it tackles contemporary issues while also using older ideological toolkits (like materialistic analysis)   which brings me to the probem of the semi-public nature of the internet : leftists share content between themselves than makes sense to them, but someone who stumbles upon it while lacking the "rich points" so to speak might be bewildered. that's why i have a tough love relationships to red twitter / tumblr / tumblr : you can get interesting facts and debates, but i don't like most of the content because it is, once again, made for people who are already part of the community! it indeed turns communism into a subculture, which is the opposite of what it is supposed to be! anyways if you are interested, Red Guards Austin made a piece that talks about this called " On identity opportunism". seriously i wish every pseudo SJW who is on socialist twitter for the likes would read it.   If you have read until here, thanks !  PS : English is not my mother tongue, i hope I didn't make too many grammar mistakes.

The left today has lost empathy for the average person in the street, and is more concerned with ideological one-up-man-ship, and demonizing anyone that doesn't 1000% toe the orthodox 'social justice' line, than it is with practical political goals, or with building broad support. And an eternally outraged ideology, where *every* mis-step is a deal-breaker is a really shitty sales pitch.   We're afraid to acknowledge the massive, unprecedented progress we're made in terms of racial, gender, LGBT equality, and public attitudes to that stuff, within the last few decades, for fear of forfeiting the right to call people racist, or misogynist, or bigots, when they say something we don't like. But ironically (and I'm very aware people hate hearing it, but it's fucking true- or at the very least it's a significant contributing factor) by constantly calling people "bigots" and "racists" for petty shit, we're pushing them into the arms of *actual* racists and bigots.   But this shit is old news. "Cult of outrage" and all that. The left doesn't *want* to change, and it doesn't *want* to appeal to a wide support base- at least not more than it wants the approval of existing leftists; the back-patting we get from preaching to the choir is more important to us than getting support from the average person in the street (who's probably an evil racist misogynist *anyway*, so fuck em; why would we *want* their vote). So we keep chasing a bunch of unattainable abstractions, and going on about how "oppressed" everyone is in objectively the most equal era in history. 'The left' is too nebulous a concept to critique. This 'leftist' is simultaneously an anarchist, a marxist-leninist tankie, an antifa 'sjw' and a pseudo-marxist centrist liberal. She's a walking contradiction, which is slightly confusing.   Marx has really important criticisms about capitalism. Without that, the left has no substantial basis other than 'equality guys'. This isn't enough - we need strong economic arguments, which we have.   There have been efforts on the part of people like David Harvey to simplify critiques of capitalism, but you can't separate these concepts from the theory. Theory requires some degree of thinking unfortunately.   In some respects, marxists are pessimistic about revolution in the first world. This is because things aren't bad enough yet - we still are able to exploit the cheap labour of workers in the third world and we are yet to significantly automate labour. But this is Marx's dialectic - class struggle will inevitably arise out of poor material conditions. It's just a question of when and why.   If suddenly a marxist movement arises outside of a time of crisis, great! I have noticed an uptick in interest in Marx on youtube, meme pages (even ironically) and some politicians who have even aligned themselves with marxists before are becoming popular (e.g Jeremy Corbyn).   The socialist ice is thawing, people are starting to read theory and not accepting the prevailing ideology as commonsensical.
